<th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;">
<div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;"><a href="/wiki/Absolute_magnitude#Solar_System_bodies_.28H.29" title="Absolute magnitude">Absolute magnitude <i>(H)</i></a></div>
</th>
<td><span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6998300000000000000♠</span>0.03<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.43</span> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jpldata_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-jpldata-8">[7]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Haumea</b>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minor-planet_designation" title="Minor-planet designation">minor-planet designation</a> <b>136108 Haumea</b>, is a <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planet</a> located beyond <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>'s orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iau_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-iau-22">[21]</a></sup> It was discovered in 2004 by a team headed by <a href="/wiki/Michael_E._Brown" title="Michael E. Brown">Mike Brown</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caltech" title="Caltech">Caltech</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Palomar_Observatory" title="Palomar Observatory">Palomar Observatory</a> in the United States and independently in 2005, by a team headed by <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Luis_Ortiz_Moreno" title="José Luis Ortiz Moreno">José Luis Ortiz Moreno</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Nevada_Observatory" title="Sierra Nevada Observatory">Sierra Nevada Observatory</a> in Spain, though the latter claim has been contested. On September 17, 2008, it was recognized as a dwarf planet by the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a> (IAU) and named after <a href="/wiki/Haumea_(mythology)" title="Haumea (mythology)">Haumea</a>, the Hawaiian goddess of childbirth.</p>
<p>Haumea's mass is about one-third that of <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>, and 1/1400 that of Earth. Although its shape has not been directly observed, calculations from its <a href="/wiki/Light_curve" title="Light curve">light curve</a> indicate that it is a triaxial <a href="/wiki/Ellipsoid" title="Ellipsoid">ellipsoid</a>, with its major <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Axis_of_rotation" title="Axis of rotation">axis</a> twice as long as its minor. Its <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a> is thought to be sufficient for it to have relaxed into <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium" title="Hydrostatic equilibrium">hydrostatic equilibrium</a>, making it a dwarf planet. Haumea's elongated shape together with its rapid rotation, high density, and high <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a> (from a surface of crystalline water ice), are thought to be the consequences of a <a href="/wiki/Impact_event" title="Impact event">giant collision</a>, which left Haumea the largest member of a <a href="/wiki/Collisional_family" title="Collisional family">collisional family</a> that includes several large <a href="/wiki/Trans-Neptunian_object" title="Trans-Neptunian object">trans-Neptunian objects</a> (TNOs) and Haumea's two known moons, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hi%CA%BBiaka_(moon)" title="Hiʻiaka (moon)">Hiʻiaka</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Namaka_(moon)" title="Namaka (moon)">Namaka</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Haumea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery">Discovery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Haumea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Discovery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Controversy_over_the_discovery_of_Haumea" title="Controversy over the discovery of Haumea">Controversy over the discovery of Haumea</a></div>
<p>Two teams claim credit for the discovery of Haumea. Mike Brown and his team at Caltech discovered Haumea in December 2004 on images they had taken on May 6, 2004. On July 20, 2005, they published an online abstract of a report intended to announce the discovery at a conference in September 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trail_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-trail-23">[22]</a></sup> At around this time, José Luis Ortiz Moreno and his team at the Instituto de Astrofísica de Andalucía at Sierra Nevada Observatory in Spain found Haumea on images taken on March 7–10, 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sanz_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sanz-24">[23]</a></sup> Ortiz emailed the <a href="/wiki/Minor_Planet_Center" title="Minor Planet Center">Minor Planet Center</a> with their discovery on the night of July 27, 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sanz_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sanz-24">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>Brown initially conceded discovery credit to Ortiz,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup> but came to suspect the Spanish team of fraud upon learning that his observation logs were accessed from the Spanish observatory the day before the discovery announcement. These logs included enough information to allow the Ortiz team to <a href="/wiki/Precovery" title="Precovery">precover</a> Haumea in their 2003 images, and they were accessed again just before Ortiz scheduled telescope time to obtain confirmation images for a second announcement to the MPC on July 29. Ortiz later admitted he had accessed the Caltech observation logs but denied any wrongdoing, stating he was merely verifying whether they had discovered a new object.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ortiz1_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-ortiz1-26">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>IAU protocol is that discovery credit for a <a href="/wiki/Minor_planet" title="Minor planet">minor planet</a> goes to whoever first submits a report to the MPC (<a href="/wiki/Minor_Planet_Center" title="Minor Planet Center">Minor Planet Center</a>) with enough positional data for a decent determination of its orbit, and that the credited discoverer has priority in choosing a name. However, the IAU announcement on September 17, 2008, that Haumea had been accepted as a dwarf planet, did not mention a discoverer. The location of discovery was listed as the Sierra Nevada Observatory of the Spanish team,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usgs_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-marsden_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-marsden-28">[27]</a></sup> but the chosen name, Haumea, was the Caltech proposal; Ortiz's team had proposed "Ataecina", named for <a href="/wiki/Ataegina" title="Ataegina">the ancient Iberian goddess of Spring</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sanz_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sanz-24">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Haumea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Until it was given a permanent name, the Caltech discovery team used the nickname "<a href="/wiki/Santa_Claus" title="Santa Claus">Santa</a>" among themselves, because they had discovered Haumea on December 28, 2004, just after Christmas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Santa2005_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Santa2005-29">[28]</a></sup> The Spanish team were the first to file a claim for discovery to the <a href="/wiki/Minor_Planet_Center" title="Minor Planet Center">Minor Planet Center</a>, in July 2005. On July 29, 2005, Haumea was given the <a href="/wiki/Provisional_designation_in_astronomy" title="Provisional designation in astronomy">provisional designation</a> <b>2003 EL<sub>61</sub></b>, based on the date of the Spanish discovery image. On September 7, 2006, it was numbered and admitted into the official minor planet catalogue as (136108) 2003 EL<sub>61</sub>.</p>
<p>Following <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Committee_on_Small_Body_Nomenclature" title="Committee on Small Body Nomenclature">guidelines</a> established by the IAU that <a href="/wiki/Classical_Kuiper_belt_object" title="Classical Kuiper belt object">classical Kuiper belt objects</a> be given names of mythological beings associated with creation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU-Naming_of_Minor_planets_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAU-Naming_of_Minor_planets-30">[29]</a></sup> in September 2006 the Caltech team submitted formal names from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hawaiian_mythology" title="Hawaiian mythology">Hawaiian mythology</a> to the IAU for both (136108) 2003 EL<sub>61</sub> and its moons, in order "to pay homage to the place where the satellites were discovered".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mike_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-mike-31">[30]</a></sup> The names were proposed by <a href="/wiki/David_L._Rabinowitz" title="David L. Rabinowitz">David Rabinowitz</a> of the Caltech team.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iau_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-iau-22">[21]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Haumea_(mythology)" title="Haumea (mythology)">Haumea</a></i> is the matron goddess of the island of <a href="/wiki/Hawaii_(island)" title="Hawaii (island)">Hawai<span class="unicode">ʻ</span>i</a>, where the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mauna_Kea_Observatory" title="Mauna Kea Observatory">Mauna Kea Observatory</a> is located. In addition, she is identified with <a href="/wiki/Papah%C4%81naumoku" title="Papahānaumoku">Papa</a>, the goddess of the earth and wife of <a href="/wiki/W%C4%81kea" title="Wākea">Wākea</a> (space),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-craig_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-craig-32">[31]</a></sup> which is appropriate because Haumea is thought to be composed almost entirely of solid rock, without the thick ice mantle over a small rocky core typical of other known Kuiper belt objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iaunews_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-iaunews-33">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrownBarkume2007_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-BrownBarkume2007-34">[33]</a></sup> Lastly, Haumea is the goddess of fertility and childbirth, with many children who sprang from different parts of her body;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-craig_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-craig-32">[31]</a></sup> this corresponds to the swarm of icy bodies thought to have broken off the dwarf planet during an ancient collision.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrownBarkume2007_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-BrownBarkume2007-34">[33]</a></sup> The two known moons, also believed to have formed in this manner,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrownBarkume2007_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-BrownBarkume2007-34">[33]</a></sup> are thus named after two of Haumea's daughters, <a href="/wiki/Hi%CA%BBiaka" title="Hiʻiaka">Hiʻiaka</a> and <a href="/wiki/N%C4%81maka" title="Nāmaka">Nāmaka</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iaunews_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-iaunews-33">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>The proposal by the Ortiz team, Ataecina, did not meet IAU naming requirements, because Ataecina is not a creation deity. <i>(See <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ataecina#Dwarf_planet" title="Ataecina">Ataecina § Dwarf planet</a>.)</i></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Haumea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Haumea is a <a href="/wiki/Plutoid" title="Plutoid">plutoid</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planet</a> beyond Neptune's orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usgs_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-27">[26]</a></sup> Its classification as a dwarf planet means it is presumed to be massive enough to have been rounded by its own gravity into a shape in hydrostatic equilibrium, but not massive enough to have <a href="/wiki/Clearing_the_neighbourhood" title="Clearing the neighbourhood">cleared its neighbourhood</a> of similar objects. Haumea appears to have a triaxial <a href="/wiki/Ellipsoid" title="Ellipsoid">ellipsoid</a> shape resulting its rapid <a href="/wiki/Rotation" title="Rotation">rotation</a> complicated by tidal interactions with its moons. This contrasts with the simpler <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oblate_spheroid" title="Oblate spheroid">oblate</a> shape typically assumed by less rapidly rotating astronomical bodies such as the Earth, Jupiter (or a <a href="/wiki/Water_balloon" title="Water balloon">water balloon</a> tossed with moderate spin). Like the earth, Haumea is flattened at the poles (indeed much more so), but its equator, instead of being round like the Earth's, is elliptical, with two big bulges 180 degrees apart in longitude, buoyed up, as it were, by the greater centrifugal force they experience by virtue of being farther from the axis of rotation. In other words, Haumea is spinning so fast that if it spun much faster these bulges would distort into a dumbbell shape and split the planet in two.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iau_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-iau-22">[21]</a></sup> Haumea was initially listed as a classical Kuiper belt object in 2006 by the <a href="/wiki/Minor_Planet_Center" title="Minor Planet Center">Minor Planet Center</a>, but no longer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-K10H75_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-K10H75-1">[1]</a></sup> The nominal trajectory suggests that it is in the weak 7:12 <a href="/wiki/Resonant_trans-Neptunian_object" title="Resonant trans-Neptunian object">resonance with Neptune</a> (orbiting the Sun 7 times for every 12 orbits of Neptune), because its <a href="/wiki/Apsis" title="Apsis">perihelion</a> distance of 35 AU is near the <a href="/wiki/Perturbation_(astronomy)" title="Perturbation (astronomy)">limit of stability</a> with Neptune.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-candidate_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-candidate-3">[3]</a></sup> There are <a href="/wiki/Precovery" title="Precovery">precovery</a> images of Haumea dating back to March 22, 1955 from the <a href="/wiki/Palomar_Mountain" title="Palomar Mountain">Palomar Mountain</a> <a href="/wiki/Digitized_Sky_Survey" title="Digitized Sky Survey">Digitized Sky Survey</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buie_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Buie-2">[2]</a></sup> Further observations of the orbit will be required to verify its dynamic status.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbit_and_rotation">Orbit and rotation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Haumea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Orbit and rotation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

</div>
</div>
<p>Haumea has an <a href="/wiki/Orbital_period" title="Orbital period">orbital period</a> of 284 Earth years, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelion" title="Perihelion">perihelion</a> of 35 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomical_Units" title="Astronomical Units">AU</a>, and an <a href="/wiki/Orbital_inclination" title="Orbital inclination">orbital inclination</a> of 28°.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jpldata_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-jpldata-8">[7]</a></sup> It passed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aphelion" title="Aphelion">aphelion</a> in early 1992,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horizons_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Horizons-21">[20]</a></sup> and is currently more than 50 AU from the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AstDys_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-AstDys-20">[19]</a></sup> Haumea's orbit has a slightly greater <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentricity</a> than that of the other members of <a href="/wiki/Haumea_family" title="Haumea family">its collisional family</a>. This is thought to be due to Haumea's weak 7:12 orbital resonance with Neptune gradually modifying its initial orbit over the course of a billion years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrownBarkume2007_34-3"><a href="#cite_note-BrownBarkume2007-34">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-largest_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-largest-35">[34]</a></sup> through the <a href="/wiki/Kozai_mechanism" title="Kozai mechanism">Kozai effect</a>, which allows the exchange of an orbit's inclination for increased eccentricity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrownBarkume2007_34-4"><a href="#cite_note-BrownBarkume2007-34">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nesvorny2001_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nesvorny2001-36">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuchner2002_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kuchner2002-37">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>With a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visual_magnitude" title="Visual magnitude">visual magnitude</a> of 17.3,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AstDys_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-AstDys-20">[19]</a></sup> Haumea is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_the_brightest_KBOs" title="List of the brightest KBOs">third-brightest object</a> in the Kuiper belt after Pluto and <a href="/wiki/Makemake" title="Makemake">Makemake</a>, and easily observable with a large amateur telescope.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rabinowitz2005_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-Rabinowitz2005-10">[9]</a></sup> However, because the planets and most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Small_Solar_System_bodies" title="Small Solar System bodies">small Solar System bodies</a> share a <a href="/wiki/Invariable_plane" title="Invariable plane">common orbital alignment</a> from their <a href="/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System" title="Formation and evolution of the Solar System">formation</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Protoplanetary_disk" title="Protoplanetary disk">primordial disk</a> of the Solar System, most early surveys for distant objects focused on the projection on the sky of this common plane, called the <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TrujilloBrown2003_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-TrujilloBrown2003-38">[37]</a></sup> As the region of sky close to the ecliptic became well explored, later sky surveys began looking for objects that had been dynamically excited into orbits with higher inclinations, as well as more distant objects, with slower <a href="/wiki/Mean_motion" title="Mean motion">mean motions</a> across the sky.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown2004_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown2004-39">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwamb2008_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schwamb2008-40">[39]</a></sup> These surveys eventually covered the location of Haumea, with its high orbital inclination and current position far from the ecliptic.</p>
<p>Haumea displays large fluctuations in brightness over a period of 3.9 hours, which can only be explained by a rotational period of this length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AFP2009_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-AFP2009-41">[40]</a></sup> This is faster than any other known equilibrium body in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>, and indeed faster than any other known body larger than 100 km in diameter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rabinowitz2005_10-4"><a href="#cite_note-Rabinowitz2005-10">[9]</a></sup> This rapid rotation is thought to have been caused by the impact that created its satellites and collisional family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrownBarkume2007_34-5"><a href="#cite_note-BrownBarkume2007-34">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_characteristics">Physical characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Haumea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Physical characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;">

<ul>

</ul>
<span style="margin-left:-0.125em">]</span></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Because Haumea has moons, the mass of the system can be calculated from their orbits using <a href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_laws_of_planetary_motion#.23Third_law" title="Kepler's laws of planetary motion">Kepler's third law</a>. The result is <span class="nowrap">4.2<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">21</span></sup> kg,</span> 28% the mass of the Plutonian system and 6% that of the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>. Nearly all of this mass is in Haumea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RagozzineBrown2009_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-RagozzineBrown2009-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown2005_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown2005-42">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Size.2C_shape.2C_and_composition">Size, shape, and composition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Haumea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Size, shape, and composition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The size of a Solar System object can be deduced from its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optical_magnitude" title="Optical magnitude">optical magnitude</a>, its distance, and its <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a>. Objects appear bright to Earth observers either because they are large or because they are highly reflective. If their reflectivity (albedo) can be ascertained, then a rough estimate can be made of their size. For most distant objects, the albedo is unknown, but Haumea is large and bright enough for its <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">thermal emission</a> to be measured, which has given an approximate value for its albedo and thus its size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spitzer_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-spitzer-14">[13]</a></sup> However, the calculation of its dimensions is complicated by its rapid rotation. The <a href="/wiki/Rigid_body_dynamics" title="Rigid body dynamics">rotational physics</a> of <a href="/wiki/Plasticity_(physics)" title="Plasticity (physics)">deformable bodies</a> predicts that over as little as a hundred days,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rabinowitz2005_10-5"><a href="#cite_note-Rabinowitz2005-10">[9]</a></sup> a body rotating as rapidly as Haumea will have been distorted into the <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium" title="Hydrostatic equilibrium">equilibrium form</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Ellipsoid#Rotational_equilibrium" title="Ellipsoid">triaxial ellipsoid</a>. It is thought that most of the fluctuation in Haumea's brightness is caused not by local differences in albedo but by the alternation of the side view and end view as seen from Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rabinowitz2005_10-6"><a href="#cite_note-Rabinowitz2005-10">[9]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Haumea.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="427" data-file-width="480" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Haumea.svg/220px-Haumea.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Haumea.svg/330px-Haumea.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Haumea.svg/440px-Haumea.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Haumea.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The calculated ellipsoid shape of Haumea, 1,960×1,518×996 km (assuming an <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a> of 0.73). At the left are the minimum and maximum equatorial silhouettes (1,960×996 and 1,518×996 km); at the right is the view from the <a href="/wiki/Geographical_pole" title="Geographical pole">pole</a> (1,960×1,518 km).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The rotation and amplitude of Haumea's <a href="/wiki/Light_curve#Planetology" title="Light curve">light curve</a> place strong constraints on its composition. If Haumea had a low <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> like Pluto, with a thick mantle of <a href="/wiki/Volatiles" title="Volatiles">ice</a> over a small <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">rocky</a> core, its rapid rotation would have elongated it to a greater extent than the fluctuations in its brightness allow. Such considerations constrain its density to a range of 2.6–3.3 g/cm<sup>3</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rabinowitz2005_10-7"><a href="#cite_note-Rabinowitz2005-10">[9]</a></sup> By comparison, the Moon, which is rocky, has a density of 3.3 g/cm<sup>3</sup>, whereas Pluto, which is typical of icy objects in the Kuiper belt, has a density of 1.86 g/cm<sup>3</sup>. Haumea's possible density covers the values for <a href="/wiki/Silicate_minerals" title="Silicate minerals">silicate minerals</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Olivine" title="Olivine">olivine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pyroxene" title="Pyroxene">pyroxene</a>, which make up many of the <a href="/wiki/Planetary_differentiation" title="Planetary differentiation">rocky objects</a> in the Solar System. This suggests that the bulk of Haumea is rock covered with a relatively thin layer of ice. A thick ice mantle more typical of Kuiper belt objects may have been blasted off during the impact that formed the Haumean collisional family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrownBarkume2007_34-6"><a href="#cite_note-BrownBarkume2007-34">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>The denser the object in hydrostatic equilibrium, the more spherical it must be for a given rotational period, placing constraints on Haumea's possible dimensions. Fitting its accurately known mass, its rotation, and its inferred density to an equilibrium ellipsoid predicts that Haumea is approximately the diameter of Pluto along its longest axis and about half that at its poles. Because no observations of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asteroid_occultation" title="Asteroid occultation">occultations</a> of stars by Haumea or occultations of the dwarf planet with its moons have yet been made, direct, precise measurements of its dimensions, like those that were made for Pluto, do not yet exist.</p>
<p>Several ellipsoid-model calculations of Haumea's dimensions have been made. The first model produced after Haumea's discovery was calculated from <a href="/wiki/Observatory#Ground-based_observatories" title="Observatory">ground-based</a> observations of Haumea's <a href="/wiki/Light_curve" title="Light curve">light curve</a> at <a href="/wiki/Visible_spectrum" title="Visible spectrum">optical</a> wavelengths: it provided a total length of 1,960 to 2,500 km and a <a href="/wiki/Visible_spectrum" title="Visible spectrum">visual</a> <a href="/wiki/Albedo#Astronomical_albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a> (p<sub>v</sub>) greater than 0.6.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rabinowitz2005_10-8"><a href="#cite_note-Rabinowitz2005-10">[9]</a></sup> The most likely shape is a triaxial ellipsoid with approximate dimensions of 2,000 x 1,500 x 1,000 km, with an albedo of 0.71.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rabinowitz2005_10-9"><a href="#cite_note-Rabinowitz2005-10">[9]</a></sup> Observations by the <a href="/wiki/Spitzer_Space_Telescope" title="Spitzer Space Telescope">Spitzer Space Telescope</a> give a diameter of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7006115000000000000♠</span>1,150<span style="margin-left:0.3em;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:85%;text-align:right">+250<br/>
−100</span></span> km</span> and an albedo of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6999840000000000000♠</span>0.84<span style="margin-left:0.3em;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:85%;text-align:right">+0.1<br/>
−0.2</span></span></span>, from <a href="/wiki/Photometry_(astronomy)" title="Photometry (astronomy)">photometry</a> at <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> wavelengths of 70 μm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spitzer_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-spitzer-14">[13]</a></sup> Subsequent light-curve analyses have suggested an equivalent circular diameter of 1,450 km.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lacerda2007_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lacerda2007-43">[42]</a></sup> In 2010 an analysis of measurements taken by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herschel_Space_Telescope" title="Herschel Space Telescope">Herschel Space Telescope</a> together with the older Spitzer Telescope measurements yielded a new estimate of the equivalent diameter of Haumea—about 1300 km.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lellouch2010_12-3"><a href="#cite_note-Lellouch2010-12">[11]</a></sup> These independent size estimates overlap at an average <a href="/wiki/Geometric_mean" title="Geometric mean">geometric mean</a> diameter of roughly 1,400 km. This makes Haumea one of the largest trans-Neptunian objects discovered,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spitzer_14-4"><a href="#cite_note-spitzer-14">[13]</a></sup> smaller than <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>, probably <a href="/wiki/Makemake" title="Makemake">Makemake</a>, and possibly <span class="nowrap"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2007_OR10" title="2007 OR10">2007 OR<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">10</span></span></a></span>, and larger than <a href="/wiki/90377_Sedna" title="90377 Sedna">Sedna</a>, <a href="/wiki/50000_Quaoar" title="50000 Quaoar">Quaoar</a>, and <a href="/wiki/90482_Orcus" title="90482 Orcus">Orcus</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surface">Surface</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Haumea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Surface">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2005, the <a href="/wiki/Gemini_Observatory" title="Gemini Observatory">Gemini</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Keck_Observatory" title="Keck Observatory">Keck</a> telescopes obtained <a href="/wiki/Spectrum" title="Spectrum">spectra</a> of Haumea which showed strong crystalline <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">water ice</a> features similar to the surface of Pluto's moon <a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">Charon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trujillo_2006_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Trujillo_2006-17">[16]</a></sup> This is peculiar, because crystalline ice forms at temperatures above 110 K, whereas Haumea's surface temperature is below 50 K, a temperature at which <a href="/wiki/Amorphous_ice" title="Amorphous ice">amorphous ice</a> is formed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trujillo_2006_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-Trujillo_2006-17">[16]</a></sup> In addition, the structure of crystalline ice is unstable under the constant rain of <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a> and energetic particles from the Sun that strike trans-Neptunian objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trujillo_2006_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-Trujillo_2006-17">[16]</a></sup> The timescale for the crystalline ice to revert to amorphous ice under this bombardment is on the order of ten million years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gemini-Charon_ice_machine_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gemini-Charon_ice_machine-44">[43]</a></sup> yet trans-Neptunian objects have been in their present cold-temperature locations for timescales of billions of years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-largest_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-largest-35">[34]</a></sup> Radiation damage should also redden and darken the surface of trans-Neptunian objects where the common surface materials of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_molecule" title="Organic molecule">organic</a> ices and <a href="/wiki/Tholin" title="Tholin">tholin-like</a> compounds are present, as is the case with Pluto. Therefore, the spectra and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colour_index" title="Colour index">colour</a> suggest Haumea and its family members have undergone recent resurfacing that produced fresh ice. However, no plausible resurfacing mechanism has been suggested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rabinowitz2008_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rabinowitz2008-19">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>Haumea is as bright as snow, with an albedo in the range of 0.6–0.8, consistent with crystalline ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rabinowitz2005_10-10"><a href="#cite_note-Rabinowitz2005-10">[9]</a></sup> Other large TNOs such as <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a> appear to have albedos as high or higher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown2006xsize_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown2006xsize-45">[44]</a></sup> Best-fit modeling of the surface spectra suggested that 66% to 80% of the Haumean surface appears to be pure crystalline water ice, with one contributor to the high albedo possibly <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_cyanide" title="Hydrogen cyanide">hydrogen cyanide</a> or <a href="/wiki/Silicate_minerals#Phyllosilicates" title="Silicate minerals">phyllosilicate clays</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trujillo_2006_17-4"><a href="#cite_note-Trujillo_2006-17">[16]</a></sup> Inorganic cyanide salts such as copper potassium cyanide may also be present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trujillo_2006_17-5"><a href="#cite_note-Trujillo_2006-17">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, further studies of the visible and near infrared spectra suggest a homogeneous surface covered by an intimate 1:1 mixture of amorphous and crystalline ice, together with no more than 8% organics. The absence of ammonia hydrate excludes cryovolcanism and the observations confirm that the collisional event must have happened more than 100 million years ago, in agreement with the dynamic studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pinilla-Alonso2009_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pinilla-Alonso2009-46">[45]</a></sup> The absence of measurable <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> in the spectra of Haumea is consistent with a warm <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">collisional history</a> that would have removed such <a href="/wiki/Volatiles" title="Volatiles">volatiles</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trujillo_2006_17-6"><a href="#cite_note-Trujillo_2006-17">[16]</a></sup> in contrast to <a href="/wiki/Makemake" title="Makemake">Makemake</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tegler07_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tegler07-47">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to the large fluctuations in Haumea's light curve due to the body's shape, which affect all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colour_index" title="Colour index">colours</a> equally, smaller independent colour variations seen in both visible and near-infrared wavelengths show a region on the surface that differs both in colour and in albedo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hour_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-hour-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lacerda2009_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lacerda2009-48">[47]</a></sup> More specifically, a large dark red area on Haumea's bright white surface was seen in September 2009, possibly an impact feature, which indicates an area rich in minerals and organic (carbon-rich) compounds, or possibly a higher proportion of crystalline ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AFP2009_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-AFP2009-41">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SpaceCom-Haumea_dark_spot_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-SpaceCom-Haumea_dark_spot-49">[48]</a></sup> Thus Haumea may have a mottled surface reminiscent of Pluto, if not as extreme.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Moons">Moons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Haumea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Moons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Haumea" title="Moons of Haumea">Moons of Haumea</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2003EL61art.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="470" data-file-width="470" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/2003EL61art.jpg/220px-2003EL61art.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/2003EL61art.jpg/330px-2003EL61art.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/2003EL61art.jpg/440px-2003EL61art.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2003EL61art.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Artist's conception of Haumea with its moons <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hi%CA%BBiaka_(moon)" title="Hiʻiaka (moon)">Hiʻiaka</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Namaka_(moon)" title="Namaka (moon)">Namaka</a>. The moons are much more distant than depicted here.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Two small <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">satellites</a> have been discovered orbiting Haumea, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hi%CA%BBiaka_(moon)" title="Hiʻiaka (moon)">(136108) Haumea I Hiʻiaka</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Namaka_(moon)" title="Namaka (moon)">(136108) Haumea II Namaka</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usgs_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-27">[26]</a></sup> Brown's team discovered both in 2005, through observations of Haumea using the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/W.M._Keck_Observatory" title="W.M. Keck Observatory">W.M. Keck Observatory</a>.</p>
<p>Hiʻiaka, at first nicknamed "<a href="/wiki/Rudolph_the_Red-Nosed_Reindeer" title="Rudolph the Red-Nosed Reindeer">Rudolph</a>" by the Caltech team,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chang2007-Iceball_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chang2007-Iceball-50">[49]</a></sup> was discovered January 26, 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown2005-AJL632_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown2005-AJL632-51">[50]</a></sup> It is the outer and, at roughly 310 km in diameter, the larger and brighter of the two, and orbits Haumea in a nearly circular path every 49 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown2006-discovery_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown2006-discovery-52">[51]</a></sup> Strong absorption features at 1.5 and 2 <a href="/wiki/Micrometre" title="Micrometre">micrometres</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> spectrum are consistent with nearly pure crystalline water ice covering much of the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barkume2006_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barkume2006-53">[52]</a></sup> The unusual spectrum, along with similar absorption lines on Haumea, led Brown and colleagues to conclude that capture was an unlikely model for the system's formation, and that the Haumean moons must be fragments of Haumea itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-largest_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-largest-35">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>Namaka, the smaller, inner satellite of Haumea, was discovered on June 30, 2005,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAUC_8636_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAUC_8636-54">[53]</a></sup> and nicknamed "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blitzen_the_Reindeer" title="Blitzen the Reindeer">Blitzen</a>". It is a tenth the mass of Hi<span class="unicode">ʻ</span>iaka, orbits Haumea in 18 days in a highly elliptical, <a href="/wiki/Osculating_orbit" title="Osculating orbit">non-Keplerian</a> orbit, and as of 2008<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Haumea&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> is inclined 13° from the larger moon, which <a href="/wiki/Perturbation_(astronomy)" title="Perturbation (astronomy)">perturbs</a> its orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RagozzineDPS08_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-RagozzineDPS08-55">[54]</a></sup> The relatively large eccentricities together with the mutual inclination of the orbits of the satellites are unexpected as they should have been damped by the <a href="/wiki/Tidal_acceleration" title="Tidal acceleration">tidal effects</a>. A relatively recent passage by a 3:1 resonance might explain the current excited orbits of the Haumean moons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown2009_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brown2009-13">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>At present, the orbits of the Haumean moons appear almost exactly edge-on from Earth, with Namaka periodically <a href="/wiki/Occultation" title="Occultation">occulting</a> Haumea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU8949_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAU8949-56">[55]</a></sup> Observation of such transits would provide precise information on the size and shape of Haumea and its moons,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-events09_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-events09-57">[56]</a></sup> as happened in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_eclipses_on_Pluto" title="Solar eclipses on Pluto">the late 1980s with Pluto and Charon.</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McFadden2007_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-McFadden2007-58">[57]</a></sup> The tiny change in brightness of the system during these occultations will require at least a <a href="/wiki/Optical_telescope#Optical_Research_telescopes" title="Optical telescope">medium</a>-<a href="/wiki/Aperture" title="Aperture">aperture</a> <a href="/wiki/List_of_optical_telescopes" title="List of optical telescopes">professional telescope</a> for detection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-events09_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-events09-57">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FabryckyDPS08_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FabryckyDPS08-59">[58]</a></sup> Hiʻiaka last occulted Haumea in 1999, a few years before discovery, and will not do so again for some 130 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shadows_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-shadows-60">[59]</a></sup> However, in a situation unique among <a href="/wiki/Regular_moon" title="Regular moon">regular satellites</a>, Namaka's orbit is being greatly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precession_(astronomy)#Anomalistic_precession" title="Precession (astronomy)">torqued</a> by Hiʻiaka, preserving the viewing angle of Namaka–Haumea transits for several more years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RagozzineDPS08_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-RagozzineDPS08-55">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-events09_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-events09-57">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FabryckyDPS08_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-FabryckyDPS08-59">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Collisional_family">Collisional family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Haumea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Collisional family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Haumea_family" title="Haumea family">Haumea family</a></div>
<p>Haumea is the largest member of its <a href="/wiki/Collisional_family" title="Collisional family">collisional family</a>, a group of astronomical objects with similar physical and orbital characteristics thought to have formed when a larger progenitor was shattered by an impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrownBarkume2007_34-7"><a href="#cite_note-BrownBarkume2007-34">[33]</a></sup> This family is the first to be identified among TNOs and includes—beside Haumea and its moons—<span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(55636)_2002_TX300" title="(55636) 2002 TX300">(55636) 2002 TX<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">300</span></span></a></span> (≈364 km), <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(24835)_1995_SM55" title="(24835) 1995 SM55">(24835) 1995 SM<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">55</span></span></a></span> (≈174 km), <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(19308)_1996_TO66" title="(19308) 1996 TO66">(19308) 1996 TO<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">66</span></span></a></span> (≈200 km), <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(120178)_2003_OP32" title="(120178) 2003 OP32">(120178) 2003 OP<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">32</span></span></a></span> (≈230 km), and <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(145453)_2005_RR43" title="(145453) 2005 RR43">(145453) 2005 RR<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">43</span></span></a></span> (≈252 km).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-candidate_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-candidate-3">[3]</a></sup> Brown and colleagues proposed that the family were a direct product of the impact that removed Haumea's ice mantle,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrownBarkume2007_34-8"><a href="#cite_note-BrownBarkume2007-34">[33]</a></sup> but a second proposal suggests a more complicated origin: that the material ejected in the initial collision instead coalesced into a large moon of Haumea, which was later shattered in a second collision, dispersing its shards outwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchlichtingSari2009_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-SchlichtingSari2009-61">[60]</a></sup> This second scenario appears to produce a dispersion of velocities for the fragments that is more closely matched to the measured velocity dispersion of the family members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchlichtingSari2009_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-SchlichtingSari2009-61">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>The presence of the collisional family could imply that Haumea and its "offspring" might have originated in the <a href="/wiki/Scattered_disc" title="Scattered disc">scattered disc</a>. In today's sparsely populated Kuiper belt, the chance of such a collision occurring over the age of the Solar System is less than 0.1 percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-disc_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-disc-62">[61]</a></sup> The family could not have formed in the denser primordial Kuiper belt because such a close-knit group would have been disrupted by <a href="/wiki/Neptune#Formation_and_migration" title="Neptune">Neptune's migration</a> into the belt—the believed cause of the belt's current low density.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-disc_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-disc-62">[61]</a></sup> Therefore, it appears likely that the dynamic scattered disc region, in which the possibility of such a collision is far higher, is the place of origin for the object that generated Haumea and its kin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-disc_62-2"><a href="#cite_note-disc-62">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>Because it would have taken at least a billion years for the group to have diffused as far as it has, the collision which created the Haumea family is believed to have occurred very early in the Solar System's history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-candidate_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-candidate-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration">Exploration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Haumea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Exploration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>It was calculated that a flyby mission to Haumea could take 14.25 years using a Jupiter gravity assist, based on a launch date of 25 September 2025. Haumea would be 48.18 AU from the Sun when the spacecraft arrives. A flight time of 16.45 years can be achieved with launch dates on 1 November 2026, 23 September 2037 and 29 October 2038.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGranaghan_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-McGranaghan-63">[62]</a></sup> Haumea could become a target for an exploration mission,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poncyb_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Poncyb-64">[63]</a></sup> and an example of this work is a preliminary study on a probe to Haumea and its moons (at 35–51 AU).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup> Probe mass, power source, and propulsion systems are key technology areas for this type of mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Poncyb_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Poncyb-64">[63]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Haumea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">

<li id="cite_note-K10H75-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-K10H75_1-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-K10H75_1-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://www.minorplanetcenter.org/mpec/K10/K10H75.html" rel="nofollow">"MPEC 2010-H75 : DISTANT MINOR PLANETS (2010 MAY 14.0 TT)"</a> <span style="font-size:85%;">(<a class="external text" href="http://cfa-www.harvard.edu/iau/mpec/K06/K06X45.html" rel="nofollow">2006 provisional Cubewano listing</a>)</span>. <li><a class="external text" href="http://www.johnstonsarchive.net/astro/astmoons/am-136108.html" rel="nofollow">(136108) Haumea, Hiʻiaka, and Namaka</a> at Johnston's Archive.com (updated September 17, 2008)</li>

<li><a class="external text" href="http://twitpic.com/59rbgj" rel="nofollow">Haumea as seen on June 10, 2011</a> by Mike Brown using the 4.20 m (165 in) <a href="/wiki/William_Herschel_Telescope" title="William Herschel Telescope">WHT</a> / <a class="external text" href="http://twitpic.com/5acp9q" rel="nofollow">~0:30–3:30 dip in the brightness of Haumea+Namaka comes when Namaka crosses Haumea</a> (Hiʻiaka, the outer moon, is blended in the images, but it rotates every 4.5 hr and adds a little variation)</li>
</ul>
